<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="learning">Learning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#learning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Although learning ability is not seriously altered with age, other factors can interfere with the older person's ability to learn, including motivation, attention span, delayed transmission of information to the brain, perceptual deficits, and illness. Older persons may display less readiness to learn and depend on previous experience for solutions to problems rather than experiment with new problem-solving techniques. Differences in the intensity and duration of the older person's physiologic arousal may make it more difficult to extinguish previous responses and acquire new material. The early phases of the learning process tend to be more difficult for older persons than younger individuals; however, after a longer early phase, they are then able to keep equal pace. Learning occurs best when the new information is related to previously learned information. Although little difference is apparent between the old and young in verbal or abstract ability, older persons do show some difficulty with perceptual motor tasks. Some evidence indicates a tendency toward simple association rather than analysis. Because it is generally a greater problem to learn new habits when old habits exist and must be unlearned, relearned, or modified, older persons with many years of history may have difficulty in this area.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-99ea021d.jpeg" alt="img-63.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="key-concept">KEY CONCEPT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#key-concept" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Older adults maintain the capacity to learn, although a variety of factors can easily interfere with the learning process.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="attention-span">Attention Span<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#attention-span" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Older adults demonstrate a decrease in vigilance performance (i.e., the ability to retain attention longer than 45 minutes). They are more easily distracted by irrelevant information and stimuli and are less able to perform tasks that are complicated or require simultaneous performance.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-872ecd4b.jpeg" alt="img-64.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="point-to-ponder">POINT TO PONDER<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#point-to-ponder" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In the past 10 years, what changes have you experienced in regard to appearance, behaviors, and attitudes? How do you feel about these changes?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="nursing-implications-of-age-related-changes">NURSING IMPLICATIONS OF AGE-RELATED CHANGES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-implications-of-age-related-changes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An understanding of common aging changes is essential to ensure competent gerontological nursing practice. Such knowledge can aid in promoting practices that enhance wellness, thereby reducing risks to health and well-being. Differentiating normal from unusual findings in older adults and the atypical presentation of illness can be invaluable in identifying pathology and obtaining treatment in a timely manner. Table 5-1 lists some nursing actions related to age-related changes.</p>
<p>TABLE 5-1 Nursing Actions Related to Age-Related Changes</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age-Related Change</th><th>Nursing Action</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Reduction in intracellular fluid</td><td>Prevent dehydration by ensuring fluid intake of at least 1,500 mL daily unless contraindicated</td></tr><tr><td>Decrease in subcutaneous fat content, decline in natural insulation</td><td>Ensure adequate clothing is worn to maintain body warmth; maintain room temperatures between 70°F (21°C) and 75°F (24°C)</td></tr><tr><td>Lower oral temperatures</td><td>Use thermometers that register below 95°F (35°C); assess baseline norm for body temperature when patient is well to be able to identify unique manifestations of fever</td></tr><tr><td>Decreased cardiac output and stroke volume; increased peripheral resistance</td><td>Allow rest between activities, procedures; recognize the longer time period required for heart rate to return to normal following a stress on the heart and evaluate the presence of tachycardia accordingly; ensure blood pressure level is adequate to meet circulatory demands by assessing physical and mental function at various blood pressure levels</td></tr><tr><td>Decreased lung expansion, activity, and recoil; lack of basilar inflation; increased rigidity of lungs and thoracic cage; less effective gas exchange and cough response</td><td>Encourage respiratory activity that promotes deep breathing; recognize that atypical symptoms and signs can accompany respiratory infection; monitor oxygen administration closely, keep oxygen infusion rate under 4 mL, unless otherwise prescribed</td></tr><tr><td>Brittleness of teeth; retraction of gingiva</td><td>Encourage daily flossing and brushing; ensure patient visits dentist annually; inspect oral cavity for periodontal disease, jagged-edged teeth, other pathologies</td></tr><tr><td>Reduced acuity of taste sensations</td><td>Observe for overconsumption of sweets and salt; be sure foods are served attractively; season food healthfully</td></tr><tr><td>Drier oral cavity</td><td>Offer fluids during meals; have patient drink before swallowing tablets and capsules, and examine oral cavity after administration to ensure drugs have been swallowed</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age-Related Change</th><th>Nursing Action</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Decreased esophageal and gastric motility; decreased gastric acid</td><td>Assess for indigestion; encourage five to six small meals rather than three large ones; advise patient not to lie down for at least 1 h following meals</td></tr><tr><td>Decreased colonic peristalsis; duller neural impulses to lower bowel</td><td>Encourage toileting schedule to provide adequate time for bowel elimination; monitor frequency, consistency, and amount of bowel movements</td></tr><tr><td>Decreased size of renal mass, number of nephrons, renal blood flow, glomerular filtration rate, tubular function</td><td>Ensure age-adjusted drug dosages are prescribed; observe for adverse responses to drugs; recognize that urine testing for glucose can be unreliable, urinary creatinine excretion and creatinine clearance are decreased, and blood urea nitrogen level is higher</td></tr><tr><td>Decreased bladder capacity</td><td>Assist patient with need for frequent toileting; ensure safety for visits to bathroom during the night</td></tr><tr><td>Weaker bladder muscles</td><td>Observe for signs of urinary tract infection; assist patient to void in upright position</td></tr><tr><td>Enlargement of prostate gland</td><td>Discuss pros and cons of testing with physician</td></tr><tr><td>Drier, more fragile vagina</td><td>Advise patient in safe use of lubricants for comfort during intercourse</td></tr><tr><td>Increased alkalinity of vaginal canal</td><td>Observe for signs of vaginitis</td></tr><tr><td>Atrophy of muscle; reduction in muscle strength and mass</td><td>Encourage regular exercise; advise patient to avoid straining or overusing muscles</td></tr><tr><td>Decreased bone mass and mineral content</td><td>Instruct patient in safety measures to prevent falls and fractures; encourage good calcium intake and exercise</td></tr><tr><td>Less prominent stages III and IV of sleep</td><td>Avoid interruptions at night; assess quantity and quality of sleep</td></tr><tr><td>Decreased visual accommodation; reduced peripheral vision; less effective vision in dark and dimly lit areas</td><td>Ensure patient has ophthalmologic examination annually; use night lights; avoid drastic changes in level of lighting; ensure objects used by patient are within visual field</td></tr><tr><td>Yellowing of lens</td><td>Avoid using shades of greens, blues, and violets together</td></tr><tr><td>Decreased corneal sensitivity</td><td>Advise patient to protect eyes</td></tr><tr><td>Presbycusis</td><td>Ensure patient has audiometric examination if problem exists; speak to patient in loud, low-pitched voice</td></tr><tr><td>Reduced capacity to sense pain and pressure</td><td>Ensure patient changes positions before tissue reddens; inspect body for problems that patient may not sense; recognize unique responses to pain</td></tr><tr><td>Reduced immunity</td><td>Protect patient from exposure to infectious diseases; recommend pneumococcal, tetanus, and annual influenza vaccinations; promote good nutritional status to improve host defenses</td></tr><tr><td>Slower metabolic rate</td><td>Advise patient to avoid excess calorie consumption</td></tr><tr><td>Altered secretion of insulin and metabolism of glucose</td><td>Advise patient to avoid high carbohydrate intake; observe for unique manifestations of hyper- or hypoglycemia</td></tr><tr><td>Flattening of dermal–epidermal junction; reduced thickness and vascularity of dermis; degeneration of elastin fibers</td><td>Use principles of pressure injury prevention</td></tr><tr><td>Skin drier</td><td>Recognize need for less frequent bathing; avoid use of harsh soaps; use skin softeners</td></tr><tr><td>Slower response and reaction time</td><td>Allow adequate time for patient to respond, process information, and perform tasks</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>?</p>
<p>KEY CONCEPT</p>
<p>By promoting positive practices in persons of all ages, nurses can help greater numbers of individuals enter late life with high levels of health and function.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="consider-this-case">CONSIDER THIS CASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#consider-this-case" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251227-ebd67afe.jpeg" alt="img-65.jpeg"></p>
<p>Mr. G is a 72-year-old retired truck driver admitted to the hospital for the treatment of acute glomerulonephritis. His height is 5 ft 11 in., and his weight is 180 lb. You note from the record that he weighed 220 lb last year and has experienced a reduction in weight at each of his monthly physician's visits. Although he has a moderate degree of chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, he continues to smoke one pack of cigarettes daily. He has varicosities on both lower extremities and hemorrhoids. Mr. G is coherent and responds appropriately. His wife comments that he has always had a sharp mind, although in the past few years he has become considerably quieter and less gregarious. As you observe Mr. G throughout the day, you note that he:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Becomes short of breath with minimal exertion</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Develops edema</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Has urinary hesitancy and scanty urine output</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Adds considerable salt to his food before tasting it</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Has difficulty hearing normal conversation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Moves very little when in bed</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="think-critically">THINK CRITICALLY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#think-critically" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Which signs and observations are related to normal aging and which can you attribute to pathology?</li>
<li>What factors contributed to the health conditions possessed by Mr. G?</li>
<li>Describe the risks that are high for Mr. G and list nursing measures that could minimize them.</li>
</ol>
<p>Nurses caring for older adults must realize that, despite the numerous changes commonly experienced with age, most older adults function admirably well and live normal, satisfying lives. Although nurses need to acknowledge factors that can alter function with aging, they should also emphasize the capabilities and assets possessed by older adults and assist persons of all ages in achieving a healthy aging process.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="bringing-research-to-life">BRINGING RESEARCH TO LIFE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bringing-research-to-life" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ten-years-younger-practice-of-chronic-aerobic-exercise-improves-attention-and-spatial-memory-functions-in-aging">Ten Years Younger: Practice of Chronic Aerobic Exercise Improves Attention and Spatial Memory Functions in Aging<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ten-years-younger-practice-of-chronic-aerobic-exercise-improves-attention-and-spatial-memory-functions-in-aging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Source: Noguera, C., Sanchez-Horcajo, R., Alvarez-Cazorla, D., &amp; Cimadevilla, J. M. (2019). Experimental Gerontology, 117(3), 53–60.</p>
<p>Physical activity can be particularly significant after age 60, when cognitive decline is more apparent, because aerobic exercise has been shown to change brain morphology and improve cognitive function. This study compared sportsmen and sedentary men between the ages of 60 and 79 years.</p>
<p>Spatial memory and executive function were among the cognitive functions that were superior in the sportsmen group as compared to the sedentary group. Other neuropsychological tasks were also performed better by the sportsmen.</p>
<p>Based on the positive effects of aerobic exercise on physical and cognitive function, nurses should encourage aerobic exercises for all individuals. Assessments should include a review of the exercises in which individuals engage. Positive reinforcement can be given to those who exercise. For those who do not regularly exercise, exploring the reasons, discussing the benefits, and assisting them in developing a plan that it realistic for them could assist in changing their behavior. In addition to encouraging older adults to engage in aerobic exercise, promoting this type of exercise in adults under the age of 65 can be beneficial so that this group may establish the habit of exercise that they can continue into late life.</p>
<p>Older adults should be advised that aerobic exercise benefits their minds as well as their bodies. That way, even individuals who are not bothered by muscles that are not firm might be concerned about losing cognitive function and, therefore, be more motivated to exercise.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="practice-realities">PRACTICE REALITIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#practice-realities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>You are working in an office with a group of medical doctors who have had some of the same patients in their practice for nearly two decades. Although many of their patients have aged, the physicians use basically the same approach, reorder the same medications, and include no review of psychosocial issues.</p>
<p>What could you suggest to update the practice to assure the needs of the aging patients are adequately being addressed?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="critical-thinking-exercises">CRITICAL THINKING EXERCISES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#critical-thinking-exercises" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>What efforts do you see to educate persons of all ages in practices that will foster a healthy aging experience?</li>
<li>What age-related changes can you identify in yourself and in your parents?</li>
<li>Consider recommendations that you would give young adults for promotion of a healthy aging process.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-summary">Chapter Summary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-summary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Changes at the basic cellular level impact all body systems. The atrophy of body fat, loss of tissue elasticity, and reduction in subcutaneous fat contribute to changes in the appearance of the body with age. Changes to body systems contribute to a higher prevalence in the older population of conditions such as infections, hypertension, poor dental status, indigestion, constipation, urinary frequency, prostatic enlargement, fractures, reduced vision, presbycusis, hypothermia, and hyperthermia. Basic intelligence is maintained, and there is some reduction in fluid intelligence. The ability to retain new information is reduced, and the retrieval of information from long-term memory can be slower. Learning ability is maintained, although several factors that can interfere with learning. A variety of nursing actions can be utilized to prevent and reduce the negative impact of aging changes and promote optimal health and function in older adults.</p>
